        <title>Genome assembly of the basket willow, Salix viminalis , reveals earliest stages of sex chromosome expansion | BMC Biology | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Sex chromosomes have evolved independently multiple times in eukaryotes and are therefore considered a prime example of convergent genome evolution. Sex chromosomes are known to emerge after recombination is halted between a homologous pair of chromosomes, and this leads to a range of non-adaptive modifications causing gradual degeneration and gene loss on the sex-limited chromosome. However, the proximal causes of recombination suppression and the pace at which degeneration subsequently occurs remain unclear. Here, we use long- and short-read single-molecule sequencing approaches to assemble and annotate a draft genome of the basket willow, Salix viminalis, a species with a female heterogametic system at the earliest stages of sex chromosome emergence. Our single-molecule approach allowed us to phase the emerging Z and W haplotypes in a female, and we detected very low levels of Z/W single-nucleotide divergence in the non-recombining region. Linked-read sequencing of the same female and an additional male (ZZ) revealed the presence of two evolutionary strata supported by both divergence between the Z and W haplotypes and by haplotype phylogenetic trees. Gene order is still largely conserved between the Z and W homologs, although the W-linked region contains genes involved in cytokinin signaling regulation that are not syntenic with the Z homolog. Furthermore, we find no support across multiple lines of evidence for inversions, which have long been assumed to halt recombination between the sex chromosomes. Our data suggest that selection against recombination is a more gradual process at the earliest stages of sex chromosome formation than would be expected from an inversion and may result instead from the accumulation of transposable elements. Our results present a cohesive understanding of the earliest genomic consequences of recombination suppression as well as valuable insights into the initial stages of sex chromosome formation and regulation of sex differentiation."/>

<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

        function OptanonWrapper() {
            var elementInside = function(candidate, element) {
                if (candidate === element) {
                    return true;
                } else if (candidate.nodeName.toLowerCase() === 'body') {
                    return false;
                } else {
                    return elementInside(candidate.parentNode, element);
                }
            };

            window.dataLayer.push({event:'OneTrustGroupsUpdated'});
            if (disclaimer) {
                if (!elementInside(document.activeElement, disclaimer)) {
                    disclaimer.querySelector('button').focus();
                }
            } else {
                document.activeElement.blur();
            }
        }

        <meta property="og:title" content="Genome assembly of the basket willow, Salix viminalis , reveals earliest stages of sex chromosome expansion"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Sex chromosomes have evolved independently multiple times in eukaryotes and are therefore considered a prime example of convergent genome evolution. Sex chromosomes are known to emerge after recombination is halted between a homologous pair of chromosomes, and this leads to a range of non-adaptive modifications causing gradual degeneration and gene loss on the sex-limited chromosome. However, the proximal causes of recombination suppression and the pace at which degeneration subsequently occurs remain unclear. Here, we use long- and short-read single-molecule sequencing approaches to assemble and annotate a draft genome of the basket willow, Salix viminalis, a species with a female heterogametic system at the earliest stages of sex chromosome emergence. Our single-molecule approach allowed us to phase the emerging Z and W haplotypes in a female, and we detected very low levels of Z/W single-nucleotide divergence in the non-recombining region. Linked-read sequencing of the same female and an additional male (ZZ) revealed the presence of two evolutionary strata supported by both divergence between the Z and W haplotypes and by haplotype phylogenetic trees. Gene order is still largely conserved between the Z and W homologs, although the W-linked region contains genes involved in cytokinin signaling regulation that are not syntenic with the Z homolog. Furthermore, we find no support across multiple lines of evidence for inversions, which have long been assumed to halt recombination between the sex chromosomes. Our data suggest that selection against recombination is a more gradual process at the earliest stages of sex chromosome formation than would be expected from an inversion and may result instead from the accumulation of transposable elements. Our results present a cohesive understanding of the earliest genomic consequences of recombination suppression as well as valuable insights into the initial stages of sex chromosome formation and regulation of sex differentiation."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/12915.jpg"/>
    
        
    </head>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Sex chromosomes have evolved independently multiple times in eukaryotes and are therefore considered a prime example of convergent genome evolution. Sex chromosomes are known to emerge after recombination is halted between a homologous pair of chromosomes, and this leads to a range of non-adaptive modifications causing gradual degeneration and gene loss on the sex-limited chromosome. However, the proximal causes of recombination suppression and the pace at which degeneration subsequently occurs remain unclear.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>Here, we use long- and short-read single-molecule sequencing approaches to assemble and annotate a draft genome of the basket willow, <i>Salix viminalis</i>, a species with a female heterogametic system at the earliest stages of sex chromosome emergence. Our single-molecule approach allowed us to phase the emerging Z and W haplotypes in a female, and we detected very low levels of Z/W single-nucleotide divergence in the non-recombining region. Linked-read sequencing of the same female and an additional male (ZZ) revealed the presence of two evolutionary strata supported by both divergence between the Z and W haplotypes and by haplotype phylogenetic trees. Gene order is still largely conserved between the Z and W homologs, although the W-linked region contains genes involved in cytokinin signaling regulation that are not syntenic with the Z homolog. Furthermore, we find no support across multiple lines of evidence for inversions, which have long been assumed to halt recombination between the sex chromosomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>Our data suggest that selection against recombination is a more gradual process at the earliest stages of sex chromosome formation than would be expected from an inversion and may result instead from the accumulation of transposable elements. Our results present a cohesive understanding of the earliest genomic consequences of recombination suppression as well as valuable insights into the initial stages of sex chromosome formation and regulation of sex differentiation.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Sex chromosomes, genomic regions associated with either males or females, have evolved independently many times in the eukaryotes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bachtrog D, Mank JE, Peichel CL, et al. Sex determination: why so many ways of doing it. PLoS Biol. 2014;12:e1001899. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pbio.1001899&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d6089e697">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Beukeboom LW, Perrin N. The evolution of sex determination. Oxford:Oxford University Press; 2014. p. 222." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d6089e700">2</a>]. Sex chromosomes come in two general forms in organisms where sex is expressed in the diploid phase of the life cycle. X-Y sex chromosomes form where the sex-specific Y chromosome is associated with males (male heterogamety), and Z-W sex chromosomes form where the sex-specific W chromosome is associated with females (female heterogamety). Both of these sex chromosome types emerge after recombination is halted between a homologous pair of chromosomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Bergero R, Charlesworth D. The evolution of restricted recombination in sex chromosomes. Trends Ecol Evol. 2009;24:94102. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.tree.2008.09.010&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d6089e703">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Muller HJ. Genetic variability, twin hybrids and constant hybrids, in a case of balanced lethal factors. Genetics. 1918;3:42299." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d6089e706">4</a>], which allows the X and Y or Z and W chromosomes to diverge from each other. Studies in systems with highly diverged, independently evolved sex chromosomes have revealed many shared genomic properties across a broad array of taxa [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bachtrog D, Mank JE, Peichel CL, et al. Sex determination: why so many ways of doing it. PLoS Biol. 2014;12:e1001899. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pbio.1001899&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d6089e709">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Beukeboom LW, Perrin N. The evolution of sex determination. Oxford:Oxford University Press; 2014. p. 222." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d6089e713">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Bachtrog D, Kirkpatrick M, Mank JE, et al. Are all sex chromosomes created equal. Trends Genet. 2011;27:3507. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.tig.2011.05.005&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d6089e716">5</a>], and sex chromosomes therefore represent an important example of convergent genome evolution.</p><p>In addition to promoting sex chromosomes divergence from one another, recombination arrest in the sex-determining region (SDR), the region harboring the sex-determining locus, leads to a range of non-adaptive consequences for the sex-limited Y or W chromosome. These include the build-up of deleterious variation and repetitive elements, as well as the loss of gene activity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bachtrog D. Y-chromosome evolution: emerging insights into processes of Y-chromosome degeneration. Nat Rev Genet. 2013;14:11324. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nrg3366&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6089e722">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Charlesworth B, Charlesworth D. The degeneration of Y chromosomes. Philos Trans R Soc Lond Ser B Biol Sci. 2000;355:156372. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1098/rstb.2000.0717&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d6089e722_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Papadopulos AS, Chester M, Ridout K, Filatov DA. Rapid Y degeneration and dosage compensation in plant sex chromosomes. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2015;112:130216. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1508454112&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6089e725">8</a>]. Due to the longstanding focus on systems with highly divergent sex chromosomes, the speed and order at which these processes occur after recombination suppression remain largely unclear.</p><p>Additionally, over evolutionary time, the non-recombining region can expand, resulting in strata or regions with differing levels of divergence between the X and Y or Z and W chromosomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bergero R, Forrest A, Kamau E, Charlesworth D. Evolutionary strata on the X chromosomes of the dioecious plant Silene latifolia: evidence from new sex-linked genes. Genetics. 2007;175:194554. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1534/genetics.106.070110&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d6089e731">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lahn BT, Page DC. Four evolutionary strata on the human X chromosome. Science. 1999;286:9647. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1126/science.286.5441.964&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d6089e731_1">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Roesti M, Kueng B, Moser D, Berner D. The genomics of ecological vicariance in threespine stickleback fish. Nat Commun. 2015;6:8767. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/ncomms9767&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d6089e731_2">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Vicoso B, Emerson JJ, Zektser Y, Mahajan S, Bachtrog D. Comparative sex chromosome genomics in snakes: differentiation, evolutionary strata, and lack of global dosage compensation. PLoS Biol. 2013;11:e1001643. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pbio.1001643.s024&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6089e731_3">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wright AE, Harrison PW, Montgomery SH, Pointer MA, Mank JE. Independent stratum formation on the avian sex chromosomes reveals inter-chromosomal gene conversion and predominance of purifying selection on the W chromosome. Evolution. 2014;68:328195. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/evo.12493&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d6089e734">13</a>]. Broadly defined, a stratum is a contiguous region along the sex chromosome where recombination suppression occurred at roughly the same time. In a nascent stratum, recombination may still occur occasionally, but the sex chromosomes are nonetheless in the earliest stages of divergence. Most importantly, although there will be substantial variance in divergence, repetitive element accumulation, and transcriptional loss, there will be more similarity for these traits within a stratum than among different strata or the pseudo-autosomal regions. Multiple strata can occur, resulting in regions along the sex chromosome with different levels of divergence [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Lahn BT, Page DC. Four evolutionary strata on the human X chromosome. Science. 1999;286:9647. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1126/science.286.5441.964&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d6089e737">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Pandey RS, Wilson Sayres MA, Azad RK. Detecting evolutionary strata on the human X chromosome in the absence of gametologous Y-linked sequences. Genome Biol Evolution. 2013;5:186371. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/gbe/evt139&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d6089e740">14</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wright AE, Moghadam HK, Mank JE. Trade-off between selection for dosage compensation and masculinization on the avian Z chromosome. Genetics. 2012;192:143345. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1534/genetics.112.145102&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d6089e743">15</a>].</p><p>Expansion of the non-recombining region and the emergence of new strata may occur gradually, in which case we might expect only partial recombination suppression in the youngest stratum, in conjunction with substantial heterogeneity in X-Y or Z-W divergence [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bergero R, Qiu S, Forrest A, Borthwick H, Charlesworth D. Expansion of the pseudo-autosomal region and ongoing recombination suppression in the Silene latifolia sex chromosomes. Genetics. 2013;194:67386. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1534/genetics.113.150755&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d6089e749">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chibalina MV, Filatov DA. Plant Y chromosome degeneration is retarded by haploid purifying selection. Curr Biol. 2011;21:14759. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cub.2011.07.045&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d6089e749_1">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Natri HM, Shikano T, Meril J. Progressive recombination suppression and differentiation in recently evolved neo-sex chromosomes. Mol Biol Evol. 2013;30:113144. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/mst035&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d6089e749_2">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Nicolas M, Marais G, Hykelova V, et al. A gradual process of recombination restriction in the evolutionary history of the sex chromosomes in dioecious plants. PLoS Biol. 2005;3:e4. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pbio.0030004&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d6089e752">19</a>]. Alternatively, some have suggested that strata form instantaneously, via large-scale inversions on the Y or W chromosome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Charlesworth D, Charlesworth B, Marais G. Steps in the evolution of heteromorphic sex chromosomes. Heredity. 2005;95:11828. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/sj.hdy.6800697&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d6089e755">20</a>], which prevent recombination between the sex chromosomes along the entirety of the reversed region.</p><p>The answers to these questions have important implications beyond sex chromosomes. Halting recombination permanently links co-adapted gene complexes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dobzhansky T. Genetics of natural populations; experiments on chromosomes of Drosophila pseudoobscura from different geographic regions. Genetics. 1948;33:588602." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d6089e762">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dobzhansky T. Genetics of the evolutionary process. New York:Columbia University Press; 1970." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d6089e762_1">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dobzhansky T, Pavlovsky O. Indeterminate outcome of certain experiments on Drosophila populations. Evolution. 1953;7:198210. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1558-5646.1953.tb00082.x&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d6089e762_2">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Dobzhansky T, Pavlovsky O. Interracial hybridization and breakdown of coadapted gene complexes in Drosophila paulistorum and Drosophila willistoni. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 1958;44:6229. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.44.6.622&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d6089e765">24</a>], also referred to as supergenes. Y and W chromosomes are thought to represent sex-specific supergenes, linking loci with sex-benefit alleles to the sex-determining locus [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bull JJ. Evolution of sex determining mechanisms. San Francisco: Benjamin-Cummings Publishing Company; 1983. p. 316." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d6089e768">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fisher RA. The evolution of dominance. Genetics. 1931;6:34568." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d6089e768_1">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rice WR. Evolution of the Y sex chromosome in animals. BioScience. 1996;46:33143. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.2307/1312947&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d6089e768_2">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wright AE, Dean R, Zimmer F, Mank JE. How to make a sex chromosome. Nat Commun. 2016;7:12087. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/ncomms12087&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d6089e771">28</a>]. Supergenes have resurfaced recently as a major potential adaptive mechanism [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Iijima T, Kajitani R, Komata S, et al. Parallel evolution of Batesian mimicry supergene in two Papilio butterflies, P. polytes and P. memnon. Science Advances. 2018;4:eaao5416. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1126/sciadv.aao5416&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d6089e774">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Joron M, Frezal L, Jones RT, et al. Chromosomal rearrangements maintain a polymorphic supergene controlling butterfly mimicry. Nature. 2011;477:2036. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nature10341&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d6089e774_1">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kpper C, Stocks M, Risse JE, et al. A supergene determines highly divergent male reproductive morphs in the ruff. Nat Genet. 2016;48:7983. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/ng.3443&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d6089e774_2">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lamichhaney S, Fan G, Widemo F, et al. Structural genomic changes underlie alternative reproductive strategies in the ruff (Philomachus pugnax). Nat Genet. 2016;48:848. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/ng.3430&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d6089e774_3">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Wang J, Wurm Y, Nipitwattanaphon M, et al. A Y-like social chromosome causes alternative colony organization in fire ants. Nature. 2013;493:6648. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nature11832&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d6089e778">33</a>], and in so doing have implicated recombination suppression as a crucial component of complex phenotypic adaptation. However, it is important to note that alternative, non-adaptive mechanisms have been suggested for recombination suppression on sex chromosomes, which can occur simply through the shifting of a sex-specific recombination hotspot away from the sex-determining locus [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ironside JE. No amicable divorce? Challenging the notion that sexual antagonism drives sex chromosome evolution. BioEssays. 2010;32:71826. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1002/bies.200900124&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d6089e781">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sun Y, Svedberg J, Hiltunen M, Corcoran P, Johannesson H. Large-scale suppression of recombination predates genomic rearrangements in Neurospora tetrasperma. Nat Commun. 2017;8:1140. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-017-01317-6&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d6089e781_1">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Branco S, Badouin H, Rodrguez de la Vega RC et al. Evolutionary strata on young mating-type chromosomes despite the lack of sexual antagonism. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America. 2017;114:70677072. doi:&#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1701658114&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d6089e781_2">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Kent TV, Uzunovi J, Wright SI. Coevolution between transposable elements and recombination. Philos Trans R Soc Lond Ser B Biol Sci. 2017;372. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1098/rstb.2016.0458&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d6089e784">37</a>].</p><p>Sex chromosomes are therefore a powerful system to understand the evolutionary consequences of recombination suppression. Furthermore, detailed studies of nascent sex chromosomes are critical if we want to understand the initial causes of recombination suppression, as well as the order and rate of the evolutionary processes that follow it. For example, recent studies of young sex chromosome systems have revealed substantial intra-specific variation in the degree of recombination suppression across populations [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dufresnes C, Bertholet Y, Wassef J, et al. Sex-chromosome differentiation parallels postglacial range expansion in European tree frogs (Hyla arborea). Evolution. 2014;68:344556. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/evo.12525&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d6089e790">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Reichwald K, Petzold A, Koch P, et al. Insights into sex chromosome evolution and aging from the genome of a short-lived fish. Cell. 2015;163:152738. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cell.2015.10.071&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d6089e790_1">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rodrigues N, Vuille Y, Loman J, Perrin N. Sex-chromosome differentiation and sex races in the common frog (Rana temporaria). Proceedings Biological Sciences. 2015;282:20142726. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1098/rspb.2014.2726&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d6089e790_2">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Wright AE, Darolti I, Bloch NI, et al. Convergent recombination suppression suggests role of sexual selection in guppy sex chromosome formation. Nat Commun. 2017;8:14251. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/ncomms14251&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d6089e793">41</a>], suggesting that the boundaries of recombination suppression are not fixed within a species.</p><p>Plants in particular are useful in the study of the earliest stages of sex chromosome formation, as many plant sex chromosomes emerged only very recently in evolutionary time [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Filatov DA. Homomorphic plant sex chromosomes are coming of age. Mol Ecol. 2015;24:32179. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/mec.13268&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d6089e799">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Geraldes A, Hefer CA, Capron A, et al. Recent Y chromosome divergence despite ancient origin of dioecy in poplars (Populus). Mol Ecol. 2015;24:324356. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/mec.13126&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d6089e799_1">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ming R, Bendahmane A, Renner SS. Sex chromosomes in land plants. Annu Rev Plant Biol. 2011;62:485514. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-arplant-042110-103914&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d6089e799_2">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Veltsos P, Cossard G, Beaudoing E, et al. Size and content of the sex-determining region of the Y chromosome in dioecious Mercurialis annua, a plant with homomorphic sex chromosomes. Genes. 2018;9. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.3390/genes9060277&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d6089e802">45</a>]. Recent studies based on next-generation sequencing of plant sex chromosomes have shown important patterns in the earliest stages of sex determination [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Akagi T, Henry IM, Tao R, Comai L. A Y-chromosome-encoded small RNA acts as a sex determinant in persimmons. Science. 2014;346:64650. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1126/science.1257225&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d6089e805">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Harkess A, Zhou J, Xu C, et al. The asparagus genome sheds light on the origin and evolution of a young Y chromosome. Nat Commun. 2017;8:1279. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-017-01064-8&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d6089e805_1">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Russell JR, Pannell JR. Sex determination in dioecious Mercurialis annua and its close diploid and polyploid relatives. Heredity. 2015;114:26271. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/hdy.2014.95&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d6089e805_2">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Tennessen JA, Wei N, Straub SCK, Govindarajulu R, Liston A, Ashman TL. Repeated translocation of a gene cassette drives sex-chromosome turnover in strawberries. PLoS Biol. 2018;16:e2006062. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pbio.2006062&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d6089e805_3">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Torres MF, Mathew LS, Ahmed I, et al. Genus-wide sequencing supports a two-locus model for sex-determination in Phoenix. Nat Commun. 2018;9:3969. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-018-06375-y&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d6089e805_4">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Wang J, Na JK, Yu Q, et al. Sequencing papaya X and Yh chromosomes reveals molecular basis of incipient sex chromosome evolution. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2012;109:137105. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1207833109&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d6089e808">51</a>]. Studies on plant sex chromosomes have also revealed the importance of haploid selection in maintaining gene activity in the non-recombining region [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chibalina MV, Filatov DA. Plant Y chromosome degeneration is retarded by haploid purifying selection. Curr Biol. 2011;21:14759. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cub.2011.07.045&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d6089e811">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Sandler G, Beaudry FEG, Barrett SCH, Wright SI. The effects of haploid selection on Y chromosome evolution in two closely related dioecious plants. Evolution Letters. 2018;2:36877. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1002/evl3.60&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d6089e815">52</a>] in the face of rapid loss of gene expression following recombination suppression [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Papadopulos AS, Chester M, Ridout K, Filatov DA. Rapid Y degeneration and dosage compensation in plant sex chromosomes. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2015;112:130216. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1508454112&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6089e818">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Ridout KE, Veltsos P, Muyle A, et al. Hallmarks of early sex-chromosome evolution in the dioecious plant Mercurialis annua revealed by de novo genome assembly, genetic mapping and transcriptome analysis. bioRxiv. 2017. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/106120&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d6089e821">53</a>].</p><p>Recent work in <i>Salix viminalis</i>, the basket willow, has revealed the presence of nascent Z-W sex chromosomes, with a highly restricted SDR [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Pucholt P, Rnnberg-Wstljung AC, Berlin S. Single locus sex determination and female heterogamety in the basket willow (Salix viminalis L.). Heredity. 2015;114:57583. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/hdy.2014.125&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d6089e830">54</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Pucholt P, Wright AE, Conze LL, Mank JE, Berlin S. Recent sex chromosome divergence despite ancient dioecy in the willow Salix viminalis. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:19912001. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx144&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d6089e833">55</a>]. The sex chromosomes of <i>Salix</i> have evolved independently from the X-Y system in the sister genus <i>Populus</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Pucholt P, Rnnberg-Wstljung AC, Berlin S. Single locus sex determination and female heterogamety in the basket willow (Salix viminalis L.). Heredity. 2015;114:57583. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/hdy.2014.125&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d6089e843">54</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Hou J, Ye N, Zhang D, et al. Different autosomes evolved into sex chromosomes in the sister genera of Salix and Populus. Sci Rep. 2015;5:9076. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/srep09076&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d6089e846">56</a>], which also exhibits very low levels of divergence between the sex chromosomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Geraldes A, Hefer CA, Capron A, et al. Recent Y chromosome divergence despite ancient origin of dioecy in poplars (Populus). Mol Ecol. 2015;24:324356. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/mec.13126&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d6089e849">43</a>]. The Salicaceae family, which includes willows and poplars, therefore presents a powerful system for studying the earliest stages of sex chromosome formation. Here, we use long- and short-read single-molecule sequencing (PacBio and 10 Genomics Chromium linked-reads approaches) in <i>S. viminalis</i> to assemble a female reference genome. Importantly, our approach allowed us to obtain phased male and female haplotypes using large, continuous haplotype scaffolds. This allows us to transcend the current limitations of short-read next-generation sequencing, which hinder the assembly of repetitive regions, common in SDRs, as well as complicate accurate phasing. Our results shed unprecedented detail on the earliest stages of sex chromosome formation and reveal that the initial stages of recombination suppression are incomplete, as would be expected from gradual selection against recombination rather than from the build-up of inversions in the SDR.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Assembly and annotation of the basket willow reference genome</h3><p>In order to gain a better understanding of the evolution and genomic architecture of the recently formed sex chromosomes in <i>Salix viminalis</i>, we sequenced and assembled the complete genome of a single diploid heterogametic female (ZW) which was previously part of a large association mapping population [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Berlin S, Trybush SO, Fogelqvist J, et al. Genetic diversity, population structure and phenotypic variation in European Salix viminalis L. (Salicaceae). Tree Genet Genomes. 2014;10:1595610. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/s11295-014-0782-5&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d6089e870">57</a>]. To this end, we used a combination of long- and short-read single-molecule sequencing strategies and generated ~19Gb of Pacific Biosciences (PacBio) long reads in a female and ~58Gb of 10 Genomics linked-reads in the same female and a male (AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S1). The full assembled genome has ~357Mb of sequence spanning 2372 scaffolds above 1kb in length, a scaffold N50 of ~1.3Mb, and 92% of the genome in scaffolds longer than 50kb. With this estimated genome size, our sequencing constitutes &gt;50 PacBio and &gt;160 10 Genomics coverage for autosomes, and &gt;25 and &gt;40 coverage of the W chromosome accounting for the hemizygous nature of the female-limited region.</p><p>Assembly quality, as assessed by whole-genome DNA and transcriptome short-read mapping, suggests a high completeness and contiguity with ~98% and ~84% of the reads, respectively, aligned to the assembled sequence (AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2). Importantly, we obtained a high proportion of properly paired reads (AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2). An initial assessment with BUSCO also identified more than 94% of complete core Embryophyta genes in the assembly (AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2). We also mapped 1987 genotype by sequencing (GBS) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Pucholt P, Rnnberg-Wstljung AC, Berlin S. Single locus sex determination and female heterogamety in the basket willow (Salix viminalis L.). Heredity. 2015;114:57583. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/hdy.2014.125&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d6089e888">54</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Pucholt P, Hallingbck HR, Berlin S. Allelic incompatibility can explain female biased sex ratios in dioecious plants. BMC Genomics. 2017;18:251. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/s12864-017-3634-5&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d6089e891">58</a>] markers in order to verify their presence and order. Consequently, our reference genome of the basket willow <i>S. viminalis</i> is essentially complete and properly assembled. Given the inherent difficulties in assembling an ancient polyploid genome such as <i>S. viminalis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Berlin S, Fogelqvist J, Lascoux M, Lagercrantz U, Rnnberg-Wstljung AC. Polymorphism and divergence in two willow species, Salix viminalis L. and Salix schwerinii E. Wolf. G3. 2011;1:387400. doi:&#xA;https://doi.org/10.1534/g3.111.000539&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d6089e901">59</a>], the relative completeness of our assembly reveals the benefits of incorporating single-molecule and long-read sequencing.</p><p>Annotation of the basket willow genome followed an in-house pipeline based on MAKER v3.00.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Holt C, Yandell M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics. 2011;12:491. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2105-12-491&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d6089e907">60</a>] that combined transcriptome data [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Pucholt P, Wright AE, Conze LL, Mank JE, Berlin S. Recent sex chromosome divergence despite ancient dioecy in the willow Salix viminalis. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:19912001. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx144&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d6089e910">55</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Darolti I, Wright AE, Pucholt P, Berlin S, Mank JE. Slow evolution of sex-biased genes in the reproductive tissue of the dioecious plant Salix viminalis. Mol Ecol. 2018;27:694708. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/mec.14466&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d6089e913">61</a>], reference proteins, and ab initio predictors. We identified 36,490 gene models, with 28,212 (77.3%) of them having functional annotation, and predicted 3469 ncRNA and 1139 tRNAs (AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S3). Finally, we also identified several families of repetitive elements which together represent ~35% of the assembly. The basket willow genome is publicly available for the community through the PopGenIE Integrative Explorer (<a href="http://popgenie.org">http://popgenie.org</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Sjdin A, Street NR, Sandberg G, Gustafsson P, Jansson S. The Populus Genome Integrative Explorer (PopGenIE): a new resource for exploring the Populus genome. New Phytologist. 2009;182:101325. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1469-8137.2009.02807.x&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d6089e927">62</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Delimitation of the SDR in the female assembly</h3><p>Differences between male and female genomes in read depth or single nucleotide polymorphim (SNP) density can be used to identify different forms of sex chromosome divergence [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Vicoso B, Emerson JJ, Zektser Y, Mahajan S, Bachtrog D. Comparative sex chromosome genomics in snakes: differentiation, evolutionary strata, and lack of global dosage compensation. PLoS Biol. 2013;11:e1001643. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pbio.1001643.s024&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6089e938">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Vicoso B, Bachtrog D. Reversal of an ancient sex chromosome to an autosome in Drosophila. Nature. 2013;499:3325. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nature12235&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d6089e941">63</a>]. In nascent sex chromosome systems, this method is particularly useful when combined with genetic mapping studies of sex-determining regions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Wright AE, Darolti I, Bloch NI, et al. Convergent recombination suppression suggests role of sexual selection in guppy sex chromosome formation. Nat Commun. 2017;8:14251. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/ncomms14251&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d6089e944">41</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Pucholt P, Wright AE, Conze LL, Mank JE, Berlin S. Recent sex chromosome divergence despite ancient dioecy in the willow Salix viminalis. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:19912001. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx144&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d6089e947">55</a>]. These methods are based on the different patterns of divergence and gene coverage differences between males and females on the sex chromosomes. In female heterogametic systems, W-specific reads are present only in females, resulting in higher female read coverage for W scaffolds. Conversely, as the W degrades, we expect a greater male read depth for the corresponding region of the Z chromosome, as females retain only one copy of the Z. Additionally, in the earliest stages of recombination suppression, we expect W regions to retain significant similarity to the Z chromosome, and therefore, females may show similar read coverage for these regions as males. However, once recombination is halted, the W is expected to accumulate polymorphisms that are not shared with the Z, and so we might expect a greater density of SNPs in females compared to males in these regions even before significant divergence lowers mapping efficiency.</p><p>In order to assess these different degrees of sex chromosome divergence, we mapped male and female short-read DNA-seq data (~69 and ~66 average sequencing coverage for females and males, respectively) to our female assembly. Because we assembled the genome of a heterogametic Z-W female, and given the relatively high levels of heterozygosity across the genome (~0.5% or 1 SNP per 200bp), we expect a proportion of divergent regions in the genome, including Z and W haplotypes, to assemble separately in different scaffolds. As this would likely bias our SNP density estimates, where regions with elevated numbers of polymorphisms would appear to be homozygous, we first constructed a non-redundant assembly by removing smaller scaffolds that showed strong evidence of sequence overlap with longer scaffolds. We then aligned our non-redundant scaffolds to the <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> genome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Tuskan GA, Difazio S, Jansson S, et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science. 2006;313:1596604. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1126/science.1128691&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d6089e956">64</a>], revealing broad synteny as expected between these sister genera (AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S1, Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#Fig1">1</a>a). In total, we anchored ~272Mb (76.4% of the full assembly) to <i>P. trichocarpa</i> chromosomes.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00808-1/MediaObjects/12915_2020_808_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00808-1/MediaObjects/12915_2020_808_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="684" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Identification of two evolutionary strata in the sex-determining region (SDR) of <i>S. viminalis</i>. Chromosome positions for <i>S. viminalis</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> are shown in Mb with the <i>S. viminalis</i> scaffold names shown on the top. The two identified strata are shown with different hues of pink and labeled above the plot. <b>a</b> Anchoring of <i>S. viminalis</i> scaffolds to the autosomal chromosome 15 of <i>P. trichocarpa</i>. Forward alignments are drawn in blue and reverse alignments are drawn in red. <b>b</b> Log<sub>2</sub> differences of normalized SNP density between <i>S. viminalis</i> females and males in non-overlapping windows of 10kb. A moving average of 25 windows is shown in the black line. The gray shaded area corresponds to the bootstrap 95% confidence interval of the autosomal data. <b>c</b> Log<sub>2</sub> differences of normalized read coverage between females and males in non-overlapping windows of 10kb. Moving average and bootstrap statistics are as in <b>b</b>. Values close to 1 indicate twice the coverage in males in comparison with females, thus potentially Z-linked</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We previously identified chromosome 15 as the sex chromosome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Pucholt P, Rnnberg-Wstljung AC, Berlin S. Single locus sex determination and female heterogamety in the basket willow (Salix viminalis L.). Heredity. 2015;114:57583. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/hdy.2014.125&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1029">54</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Pucholt P, Wright AE, Conze LL, Mank JE, Berlin S. Recent sex chromosome divergence despite ancient dioecy in the willow Salix viminalis. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:19912001. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx144&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1032">55</a>] and mapped the extent of the SDR on this chromosome (highlighted in pink, Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#Fig1">1</a>). Our results show that the five scaffolds within the SDR show significant deviations relative to the autosomal or pseudo-autosomal scaffolds of both female:male SNP density (<i>p</i>&lt;0.0001, one-sided <i>p</i> value from 10,000 permutations), indicative of the build-up of female-specific SNPs on the W, and/or female:male read coverage differences, suggesting regions of significant divergence between the Z and W chromosomes (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#Fig1">1</a>). It is important to note that because <i>S. viminalis</i> exhibits only a limited divergence between the Z and W, and our long-read assembly was based on a female sample, the assembly of the sex chromosome regions likely represents Z-W chimeras. Chimeras such as this can obscure signals of Z-W divergence, particularly if sliding windows are very large. This chimerism is evident in scaffolds 150 and 163, which both show a region of similar coverage in males and females and a region of strong female bias that likely represents W-specific genetic material (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#Fig1">1</a>). These scaffolds, in addition to scaffold 225, show the greatest deviations in the read depth between males and females and likely represent a region where recombination was first suppressed between the emerging Z and W chromosomes (stratum I). Our previous linkage mapping of the same population with GBS markers [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Pucholt P, Rnnberg-Wstljung AC, Berlin S. Single locus sex determination and female heterogamety in the basket willow (Salix viminalis L.). Heredity. 2015;114:57583. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/hdy.2014.125&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1054">54</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Pucholt P, Hallingbck HR, Berlin S. Allelic incompatibility can explain female biased sex ratios in dioecious plants. BMC Genomics. 2017;18:251. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/s12864-017-3634-5&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1057">58</a>] also placed scaffold 127 together with scaffolds 163 and 225 in the SDR (AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S2). However, the former scaffold shows far fewer differences in female:male read depth while having higher polymorphism in females relative to males. As a result, this likely represents a region where recombination has been suppressed very recently or remains partially incomplete (stratum II).</p><p>The SDR region spans a total of ~3.4Mb, or ~3.1Mb when excluding the putatively chimeric regions, and this estimate is somewhat smaller than that of our previous estimation of ~5.3Mb [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Pucholt P, Wright AE, Conze LL, Mank JE, Berlin S. Recent sex chromosome divergence despite ancient dioecy in the willow Salix viminalis. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:19912001. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx144&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1066">55</a>]. This difference is likely due to the fact that our previous estimate was based on a male assembly and included non-aligned regions on chromosome 15 of <i>P. trichocarpa</i>. In <i>Salix purpurea</i>, a close relative of <i>S. viminalis</i> with a divergence time of ~10 million years [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Zhao YJ, Liu XY, Guo R, Hu KR, Cao Y, Dai F. Comparative genomics and transcriptomics analysis reveals evolution patterns of selection in the Salix phylogeny. BMC Genomics. 2019;20:253. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/s12864-019-5627-z&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1078">65</a>], the SDR is also located on chromosome 15; however, it is much larger (&gt;10Mb) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Zhou R, Macaya-Sanz D, Rodgers-Melnick E, et al. Characterization of a large sex determination region in Salix purpurea L. (Salicaceae). Mol Gen Genomics. 2018;293:143752. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/s00438-018-1473-y&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1082">66</a>]. It has been suggested that these sex chromosomes share a common origin [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Zhou R, Macaya-Sanz D, Rodgers-Melnick E, et al. Characterization of a large sex determination region in Salix purpurea L. (Salicaceae). Mol Gen Genomics. 2018;293:143752. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/s00438-018-1473-y&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1085">66</a>], although it remains unclear whether the SDR in these two species is in the same syntenic region. In order to test whether the SDR regions overlapped between the lineages leading to these the two species, we aligned our <i>S. viminalis</i> genome assembly to the <i>S. purpurea</i> assembly. We found that all scaffolds inferred to be part of the <i>S. viminalis</i> SDR aligned to the SDR region in <i>S. purpurea</i> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#Fig2">2</a>), suggesting a shared origin, albeit with several potential rearrangements between them.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00808-1/MediaObjects/12915_2020_808_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00808-1/MediaObjects/12915_2020_808_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="645" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Alignment between <i>S. viminalis</i> and <i>S. purpurea</i> SDR regions. One-to-one orthologous alignments between <i>S. viminalis</i> scaffolds and chromosome 15 of <i>S. purpurea</i>, with forward alignments drawn in blue and reverse alignments drawn in red. The SDR region of <i>S. purpurea</i> is delimited by the gray shaded area (10.715.3Mb, from Zhou et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Zhou R, Macaya-Sanz D, Rodgers-Melnick E, et al. Characterization of a large sex determination region in Salix purpurea L. (Salicaceae). Mol Gen Genomics. 2018;293:143752. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/s00438-018-1473-y&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1130">66</a>]). <i>S. viminalis</i> scaffolds anchored to chromosome 15 of <i>P. trichocarpa</i> are highlighted in bold, those inferred to be part of the <i>S. viminalis</i> SDR are underlined, and scaffolds well anchored with several colinear markers on other chromosomes are marked with an asterisk</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Two evolutionary strata on the <i>S. viminalis</i> sex chromosomes</h3><p>It is possible to quantify divergence between the sex chromosomes by comparing <i>d</i><sub>N</sub> (a measure of non-synonymous divergence) and <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> (a measure of synonymous divergence) between males and females in the sex-linked region. To accurately estimate this divergence, we constructed 10 Genomics Chromium de novo assemblies using one individual of each sex. Fully phased diploid genotypes were obtained for 65.8% and 61.6% of the genome in our female and the male samples, respectively. Similar phasing efficiency was also achieved for chromosome 15 (AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S3) and for genes on the SDR, resulting in an average of 137 (63.7%) and 113 (52.5%) of genes phased in the female and male diploid assemblies, respectively. Our results show significantly greater <i>d</i><sub>N</sub> and <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> between stratum I and the genomic average in our female sample, but not in our male sample (genomic averages in female <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> 0.007460, female <i>d</i><sub>N</sub> 0.002080, male <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> 0.008151, male <i>d</i><sub>N</sub> 0.002261; stratum I female <i>d</i><sub>S</sub>: mean=0.012286, <i>p</i>=0.00072; female <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>: mean=0.007036, <i>p</i>=0.000077; stratum I male <i>d</i><sub>S</sub>: mean=0.008700, <i>p</i>=0.65; male <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>: mean=0.003456, <i>p</i>=0.25, based on Mann-Whitney <i>U</i> test relative to the genome, Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#Fig3">3</a>), indicating low but detectable divergence between the Z and W in this region. When stratum II is also included, the SDR shows a marginally non-significant divergence in the female (female <i>d</i><sub>S</sub>: mean=0.006402, <i>p</i>=0.89; female <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>: mean=0.004020, <i>p</i>=0.061; male <i>d</i><sub>S</sub>: mean=0.005052, <i>p</i>=0.99; male <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>: mean=0.002084, <i>p</i>=0.94, Mann-Whitney <i>U</i> test relative to the genome) despite the presence of sex-linked markers in this region (AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S2), reinforcing the conclusion that either recombination was suppressed very recently in this region, or is not yet entirely complete. These estimates are comparatively lower than those obtained in many other plant systems [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Martin H, Carpentier F, Gallina S, et al. Evolution of young sex chromosomes in two dioecious sister plant species with distinct sex determination systems. Genome Biol Evolution. 2019;11:35061. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/gbe/evz001&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1271">67</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Veltsos P, Ridout KE, Toups MA, et al. Early sex-chromosome evolution in the diploid dioecious plant Mercurialis annua. Genetics. 2019;212:81535. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1534/genetics.119.302045&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1274">68</a>]. With the exception of the higher <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> in stratum I, they are also similar to those estimated in <i>S. purpurea</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Zhou R, Macaya-Sanz D, Rodgers-Melnick E, et al. Characterization of a large sex determination region in Salix purpurea L. (Salicaceae). Mol Gen Genomics. 2018;293:143752. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/s00438-018-1473-y&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1285">66</a>]. <i>d</i><sub>N</sub> and <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> were also marginally significantly higher between the pseudo-autosomal region (PAR) and the genome in females (<i>d</i><sub>S</sub>: mean=0.009345, <i>p</i>=0.0019; <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>: mean=0.002308, <i>p</i>=0.0133, Mann-Whitney <i>U</i> test), but not in males (<i>d</i><sub>S</sub>: mean=0.007991, <i>p</i>=0.93; <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>: mean=0.002176, <i>p</i>=0.94, Mann-Whitney <i>U</i> test).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00808-1/MediaObjects/12915_2020_808_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00808-1/MediaObjects/12915_2020_808_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="897" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Comparison of polymorphisms at synonymous (<i>d</i><sub>S</sub>) and non-synonymous (<i>d</i><sub>N</sub>) sites. <b>a</b> Boxplots of d<sub>S</sub> estimates. <b>b</b> Boxplots of <i>d</i><sub>N</sub> estimates. <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> and <i>d</i><sub>N</sub> were calculated based on the coding sequence alignment of phased diploid haplotypes from one female and one male individuals in the genome (excluding chromosome 15), the pseudo-autosomal region (PAR), the sex-determining region (SDR), and the more divergent stratum I. The inset plots show the quartile distributions of <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> and <i>d</i><sub>N</sub> estimates without outliers. Significant values from the Mann-Whitney <i>U</i> test relative to the genome are indicated with asterisks: *<i>p</i>&lt;0.05; **<i>p</i>&lt;0.01; ***<i>p</i>&lt;0.001</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Phylogenetic analysis of Z-W orthologs in conjunction with outgroup species can reveal the relative timing of recombination suppression [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wright AE, Harrison PW, Montgomery SH, Pointer MA, Mank JE. Independent stratum formation on the avian sex chromosomes reveals inter-chromosomal gene conversion and predominance of purifying selection on the W chromosome. Evolution. 2014;68:328195. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/evo.12493&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1405">13</a>]. We therefore used our phased male and female haplotypes in the SDR together with orthologous genes from two closely related <i>Salix</i> species (<i>S. suchowensis</i> and <i>S. purpurea</i>) and poplar (<i>P. trichocarpa</i>). Our phylogenetic analyses provide further support for two evolutionary strata with different times since recombination suppression (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#Fig4">4</a>, AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S4). Phylogenies based on genes located in stratum I tend to show one female haplotype, corresponding to the W haplotype, clustering as an outgroup from the other three <i>S. viminalis</i> haplotypes (two male Z haplotypes and the female Z haplotype). This phylogenetic structure indicates that recombination ceased in stratum I prior to <i>S. viminalis</i> speciation. The phylogenetic structure in stratum II shows most female haplotypes clustered together with the male haplotypes, in line with more recent, or possibly partially incomplete, recombination suppression.
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00808-1/MediaObjects/12915_2020_808_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00808-1/MediaObjects/12915_2020_808_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="358" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Examples of phylogenetic trees between gametologous gene pairs in the basket willow SDR. <b>a</b>, <b>b</b> The W-linked copy of the female gametolog is more divergent and does not cluster with the other <i>S. viminalis</i> haplotypes, indicating that suppression of recombination in stratum I occurred prior to <i>S. viminalis</i> speciation. <b>c</b>, <b>d</b> The female W-linked copy clusters within the species branch suggesting that recombination has been halted more recently. Female <i>S. viminalis</i> gametologs are indicated with red squares, and male haplotypes are in blue. Trees were estimated by maximum likelihood. Bootstrap values &gt;75% are indicated with black dots on the respective nodes. The poplar (<i>P. trichocarpa</i>) ortholog was used to root the trees</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Distinct evolutionary strata are evident in many sex chromosome systems [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bergero R, Forrest A, Kamau E, Charlesworth D. Evolutionary strata on the X chromosomes of the dioecious plant Silene latifolia: evidence from new sex-linked genes. Genetics. 2007;175:194554. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1534/genetics.106.070110&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1479">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lahn BT, Page DC. Four evolutionary strata on the human X chromosome. Science. 1999;286:9647. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1126/science.286.5441.964&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1479_1">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Roesti M, Kueng B, Moser D, Berner D. The genomics of ecological vicariance in threespine stickleback fish. Nat Commun. 2015;6:8767. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/ncomms9767&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1479_2">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Vicoso B, Emerson JJ, Zektser Y, Mahajan S, Bachtrog D. Comparative sex chromosome genomics in snakes: differentiation, evolutionary strata, and lack of global dosage compensation. PLoS Biol. 2013;11:e1001643. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pbio.1001643.s024&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1479_3">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wright AE, Harrison PW, Montgomery SH, Pointer MA, Mank JE. Independent stratum formation on the avian sex chromosomes reveals inter-chromosomal gene conversion and predominance of purifying selection on the W chromosome. Evolution. 2014;68:328195. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/evo.12493&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1482">13</a>], and the mechanism behind recombination suppression, whether it is a large-scale inversion on the sex-limited chromosome [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Charlesworth D, Charlesworth B, Marais G. Steps in the evolution of heteromorphic sex chromosomes. Heredity. 2005;95:11828. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/sj.hdy.6800697&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1485">20</a>] or a more gradual suppression of recombination [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bergero R, Qiu S, Forrest A, Borthwick H, Charlesworth D.
