Expansion of the pseudo-autosomal region and ongoing recombination suppression in the Silene latifolia sex chromosomes. Genetics. 2013;194:67386. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1534/genetics.113.150755&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1488">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chibalina MV, Filatov DA. Plant Y chromosome degeneration is retarded by haploid purifying selection. Curr Biol. 2011;21:14759. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cub.2011.07.045&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1488_1">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Natri HM, Shikano T, Meril J. Progressive recombination suppression and differentiation in recently evolved neo-sex chromosomes. Mol Biol Evol. 2013;30:113144. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/mst035&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1488_2">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Nicolas M, Marais G, Hykelova V, et al. A gradual process of recombination restriction in the evolutionary history of the sex chromosomes in dioecious plants. PLoS Biol. 2005;3:e4. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pbio.0030004&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1491">19</a>], remains unclear. Crucially, males and females can differ substantially both in frequency and in location of recombination hotspots [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Burt A, Bell G, Harvey PH. Sex differences in recombination. J Evol Biol. 1991;4:25977. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1046/j.1420-9101.1991.4020259.x&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1495">69</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kong A, Thorleifsson G, Gudbjartsson DF, et al. Fine-scale recombination rate differences between sexes, populations and individuals. Nature. 2010;467:1099103. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nature09525&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1495_1">70</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lenormand T. The evolution of sex dimorphism in recombination. Genetics. 2003;163:81122." href="#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1495_2">71</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Lenormand T, Dutheil J. Recombination difference between sexes: a role for haploid selection. PLoS Biol. 2005;3:e63. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pbio.0030063&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1498">72</a>], referred to as heterochiasmy. Local sex-specific recombination rates within the genome may be important in both initial sex chromosome divergence and subsequent expansion of the non-recombining region [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wright AE, Dean R, Zimmer F, Mank JE. How to make a sex chromosome. Nat Commun. 2016;7:12087. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/ncomms12087&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1501">28</a>]. Importantly, once recombination has been halted around the SDR in the heterogametic sex, selection to maintain gene order is abolished [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Flot JF, Hespeels B, Li X, et al. Genomic evidence for ameiotic evolution in the bdelloid rotifer Adineta vaga. Nature. 2013;500:4537. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nature12326&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1504">73</a>], and selection against inversions is greatly reduced. This suggests that inversions might follow recombination suppression, as has been recently observed [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Sun Y, Svedberg J, Hiltunen M, Corcoran P, Johannesson H. Large-scale suppression of recombination predates genomic rearrangements in Neurospora tetrasperma. Nat Commun. 2017;8:1140. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-017-01317-6&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1507">35</a>], even if they are not the cause of recombination suppression initially.</p><p>If inversions are the cause of recombination suppression between the Z and W, we would expect our female assembly to be heterozygous for inversions between the Z and W chromosomes in the SDR. To identify potential structural variations between the Z and W chromosomes, we mapped the female 10 Genomics sequencing reads to the reference genome with the barcode-aware Long Ranger pipeline from 10 Genomics. With this approach, we could detect several heterozygous deletions that largely overlap with the differences in coverage between females and males, but, consistent with the conserved synteny between the homologous Z- and W-linked scaffolds (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#Fig5">5</a>, see below), we observe no evidence for inversions associated with either stratum I or stratum II (AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S4).
</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00808-1/MediaObjects/12915_2020_808_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12915-020-00808-1/MediaObjects/12915_2020_808_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="445" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Synteny analyses of Z- and W-linked resolved haplotypes. <b>a</b> Circular plots showing that scaffolds 148 and 211 are W-linked and align to the SDR of chromosome 15. From the outside to the center, (I) depicts the heatmap of log<sub>2</sub> females:males read depth in non-overlapping windows of 5kb, (II) shows the repeat proportion in non-overlapping windows of 10kb, and (III) indicates the location of annotated genes. Links between genes were computed from the best BLASTP hits and are color coded relative to the BLASTP alignment percent identity, with percent identity &gt;80% in blue and &gt;90% in red. Positions are shown in kb. <b>b</b>, <b>c</b> A highly conserved synteny between Z- and W-linked scaffolds</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>It is still possible that inversions formed within the few remaining breakpoints in between our scaffolds, which we would not be able to detect. However, it is worth noting that we observed considerable overlap in both <i>d</i><sub>S</sub> and <i>d</i><sub>N</sub> estimates (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#Fig3">3</a>) between the two strata and also the incomplete segregation of some female stratum I Z and W haplotypes (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#Fig4">4</a>, AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S4), suggesting a gradual divergence with residual or ongoing recombination in the sex chromosomes of <i>S. viminalis</i>. This gradual divergence is not consistent with a major inversion, which would result in a more similar phylogenetic signal for all Z-W orthologs within the inversion as recombination would be suppressed at the same time. Older sex chromosomes also show substantial variation in divergence within perceived strata [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Lahn BT, Page DC. Four evolutionary strata on the human X chromosome. Science. 1999;286:9647. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1126/science.286.5441.964&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1573">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wright AE, Harrison PW, Montgomery SH, Pointer MA, Mank JE. Independent stratum formation on the avian sex chromosomes reveals inter-chromosomal gene conversion and predominance of purifying selection on the W chromosome. Evolution. 2014;68:328195. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/evo.12493&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1576">13</a>]; however, the limited number of loci remaining on the oldest regions of the sex-limited chromosome complicates these analyses. In these older systems, strata may also have formed through shifts in sex-specific recombination hotspots, resulting in gradual expansions rather than large-scale inversion events.</p><p>Together, our evidence suggests that at the earliest stages of sex chromosome formation and expansion, recombination suppression is a gradual process and may result from changes in sex-specific recombination hotspots or from epigenetic variation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Metzger DCH, Schulte PM. The DNA methylation landscape of stickleback reveals patterns of sex chromosome evolution and effects of environmental salinity. Genome Biol Evol. 2018;10:77585. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/gbe/evy034&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1582">74</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Zhang W, Wang X, Yu Q, Ming R, Jiang J. DNA methylation and heterochromatinization in the male-specific region of the primitive Y chromosome of papaya. Genome Res. 2008;18:193843. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.078808.108&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1585">75</a>]. Therefore, theoretical models about local changes in heterochiasmy as a result of sexually antagonistic alleles [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Lenormand T. The evolution of sex dimorphism in recombination. Genetics. 2003;163:81122." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1588">71</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Lenormand T, Dutheil J. Recombination difference between sexes: a role for haploid selection. PLoS Biol. 2005;3:e63. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pbio.0030063&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1591">72</a>] may prove to be key to sex chromosome evolution. Alternatively, recent evidence from fungal mating-type chromosomes, analogous to sex chromosomes in many ways, has suggested non-adaptive explanations for the origin and expansion of the non-recombining region due to neutral rearrangements [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Branco S, Badouin H, Rodrguez de la Vega RC et al. Evolutionary strata on young mating-type chromosomes despite the lack of sexual antagonism. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America. 2017;114:70677072. doi:&#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1701658114&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1594">36</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Branco S, Carpentier F, Rodrguez de la Vega RC, et al. Multiple convergent supergene evolution events in mating-type chromosomes. Nature Communications. 2018;9:2000. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-018-04380-9&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1598">76</a>] or shifts in recombination hotspots [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Sun Y, Svedberg J, Hiltunen M, Corcoran P, Johannesson H. Large-scale suppression of recombination predates genomic rearrangements in Neurospora tetrasperma. Nat Commun. 2017;8:1140. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-017-01317-6&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1601">35</a>].</p><p>These non-adaptive models may also explain some of the curious intra-specific heterogeneity in the extent of sex chromosome divergence in younger systems [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dufresnes C, Bertholet Y, Wassef J, et al. Sex-chromosome differentiation parallels postglacial range expansion in European tree frogs (Hyla arborea). Evolution. 2014;68:344556. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/evo.12525&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1607">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Reichwald K, Petzold A, Koch P, et al. Insights into sex chromosome evolution and aging from the genome of a short-lived fish. Cell. 2015;163:152738. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cell.2015.10.071&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1607_1">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rodrigues N, Vuille Y, Loman J, Perrin N. Sex-chromosome differentiation and sex races in the common frog (Rana temporaria). Proceedings Biological Sciences. 2015;282:20142726. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1098/rspb.2014.2726&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1607_2">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Wright AE, Darolti I, Bloch NI, et al. Convergent recombination suppression suggests role of sexual selection in guppy sex chromosome formation. Nat Commun. 2017;8:14251. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/ncomms14251&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1610">41</a>]. If recombination suppression occurs more gradually, population-level differences in sex-specific recombination hotspots, often observed [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Kong A, Thorleifsson G, Gudbjartsson DF, et al. Fine-scale recombination rate differences between sexes, populations and individuals. Nature. 2010;467:1099103. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nature09525&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1613">70</a>], will drive different levels of divergence in the earliest stages of sex chromosomes, leading to inter-population differences in sex chromosome divergence.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Degeneration of the W chromosome</h3><p>Although studies of old, highly degenerate Y and W chromosomes have revealed the accumulation of significant repetitive DNA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Soh YQ, Alfldi J, Pyntikova T, et al. Sequencing the mouse Y chromosome reveals convergent gene acquisition and amplification on both sex chromosomes. Cell. 2014;159:80013. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cell.2014.09.052&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1624">77</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Tomaszkiewicz M, Rangavittal S, Cechova M, et al. A time- and cost-effective strategy to sequence mammalian Y chromosomes: an application to the de novo assembly of gorilla Y. Genome Res. 2016;26:53040. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.199448.115&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1627">78</a>], it remains unclear how quickly this material accumulates after recombination suppression. Additionally, the build-up of repetitive elements on the W chromosome may in itself act as a mechanism to suppress recombination with the corresponding region of the Z [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ben-Aroya S, Mieczkowski PA, Petes TD, Kupiec M. The compact chromatin structure of a Ty repeated sequence suppresses recombination hotspot activity in Saccharomyces cerevisiae. Mol Cell. 2004;15:22131. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.molcel.2004.06.002&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1630">79</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="He L, Dooner HK. Haplotype structure strongly affects recombination in a maize genetic interval polymorphic for Helitron and retrotransposon insertions. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2009;106:84106. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.0902972106&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1630_1">80</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Sasaki M, Tischfield SE, van Overbeek M, Keeney S. Meiotic recombination initiation in and around retrotransposable elements in Saccharomyces cerevisiae. PLoS Genet. 2013;9:e1003732. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pgen.1003732&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1633">81</a>]. Repetitive sequences can also trigger the recruitment of the DNA methylation and histone modification machine, and by an increase in heterochromatinization indirectly promote recombination suppression in the sex chromosomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Zhang W, Wang X, Yu Q, Ming R, Jiang J. DNA methylation and heterochromatinization in the male-specific region of the primitive Y chromosome of papaya. Genome Res. 2008;18:193843. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.078808.108&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1636">75</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li SF, Zhang GJ, Yuan JH, Deng CL, Gao WJ. Repetitive sequences and epigenetic modification: inseparable partners play important roles in the evolution of plant sex chromosomes. Planta. 2016;243:108395. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/s00425-016-2485-7&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1640">82</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhou Q, Ellison CE, Kaiser VB, Alekseyenko AA, Gorchakov AA, Bachtrog D. The epigenome of evolving Drosophila neo-sex chromosomes: dosage compensation and heterochromatin formation. PLoS Biol. 2013;11:e1001711. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pbio.1001711&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1640_1">83</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Matzke MA, Mosher RA. RNA-directed DNA methylation: an epigenetic pathway of increasing complexity. Nat Rev Genet. 2014;15:394408. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nrg3683&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1643">84</a>]. However, the difficulty associated with phasing short-read data has previously hampered efforts to study the earliest stages of sex chromosome divergence. Although it is possible to identify sex-specific transcripts from pedigrees based on inheritance through familial pedigrees [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Martin H, Carpentier F, Gallina S, et al. Evolution of young sex chromosomes in two dioecious sister plant species with distinct sex determination systems. Genome Biol Evolution. 2019;11:35061. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/gbe/evz001&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1646">67</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hough J, Hollister JD, Wang W, Barrett SC, Wright SI. Genetic degeneration of old and young Y chromosomes in the flowering plant Rumex hastatulus. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2014;111:77138. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1319227111&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1649">85</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Muyle A, Kfer J, Zemp N, Mousset S, Picard F, Marais GA. SEX-DETector: a probabilistic approach to study sex chromosomes in non-model organisms. Genome Biol Evol. 2016;8:253043. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/gbe/evw172&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1649_1">86</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Muyle A, Zemp N, Fruchard C, et al. Genomic imprinting mediates dosage compensation in a young plant XY system. Nature Plants. 2018;4:67780. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41477-018-0221-y&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1652">87</a>], this method misses non-coding sequence, making it difficult to assess whether non-coding repetitive elements are associated with the earliest stages of recombination suppression.</p><p>In order to identify a W-specific sequence, we mapped female and male sequencing reads to our female assembly. We were able to identify an additional subset of 35 scaffolds spanning ~3.3Mb and with 119 protein-coding genes (AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S5), which likely represent W-specific sequence, i.e., with a significant excess of female:male read coverage over the entire scaffold length based on genomic confidence intervals. Despite the recent origin of recombination suppression, these scaffolds show a significant enrichment of repetitive sequences in comparison with both the corresponding Z-linked portion of the SDR and the genomic average (AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Fig. S5, W genome <i>p</i>&lt;110<sup>46</sup>; W-SDR <i>p</i>=0.00058, Mann-Whitney <i>U</i> test). These results suggest that either repetitive sequence can accumulate very quickly following the arrest of recombination, or alternatively repetitive elements may in fact act to halt recombination in the absence of inversions.</p><p>The loss of recombination on the sex-limited SDR has important evolutionary effects, namely the build-up of deleterious variation and repetitive elements, as well as the loss of gene activity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bachtrog D. Y-chromosome evolution: emerging insights into processes of Y-chromosome degeneration. Nat Rev Genet. 2013;14:11324. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nrg3366&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1679">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Charlesworth B, Charlesworth D. The degeneration of Y chromosomes. Philos Trans R Soc Lond Ser B Biol Sci. 2000;355:156372. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1098/rstb.2000.0717&#xA;&#xA;." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1679_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Papadopulos AS, Chester M, Ridout K, Filatov DA. Rapid Y degeneration and dosage compensation in plant sex chromosomes. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2015;112:130216. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1508454112&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1682">8</a>]. The latter effect in particular can lead to profound differences in gene content between X and Y or Z and W chromosomes in older sex chromosome systems [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bachtrog D. Y-chromosome evolution: emerging insights into processes of Y-chromosome degeneration. Nat Rev Genet. 2013;14:11324. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nrg3366&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1685">6</a>]. Studies in other plant sex chromosomes have indicated that gene loss occurs in the SDR [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Papadopulos AS, Chester M, Ridout K, Filatov DA. Rapid Y degeneration and dosage compensation in plant sex chromosomes. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2015;112:130216. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1508454112&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1688">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Ridout KE, Veltsos P, Muyle A, et al. Hallmarks of early sex-chromosome evolution in the dioecious plant Mercurialis annua revealed by de novo genome assembly, genetic mapping and transcriptome analysis. bioRxiv. 2017. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/106120&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1691">53</a>], however, it remains unclear how quickly this occurs. Additionally, the extended haploid phase in plants may prevent loss of SDR genes expressed in the haploid phase [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chibalina MV, Filatov DA. Plant Y chromosome degeneration is retarded by haploid purifying selection. Curr Biol. 2011;21:14759. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cub.2011.07.045&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1695">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Sandler G, Beaudry FEG, Barrett SCH, Wright SI. The effects of haploid selection on Y chromosome evolution in two closely related dioecious plants. Evolution Letters. 2018;2:36877. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1002/evl3.60&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1698">52</a>].</p><p>In order to identify gene content differences between the Z and the W chromosome, we used two of the W-linked scaffolds identified above, scaffolds 148 and 211. These scaffolds align almost entirely to the SDR where read mapping coverage is male-biased (Z-linked), as would be expected for sex-linked homologous regions (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#Fig5">5</a>a). In both cases, we observed a high degree of synteny in the aligned regions, indicating that both gene content and gene order are still largely conserved between Z and W homologs, even in the most divergent region of the SDR (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#Fig5">5</a>b, c). This is likely a function of both the recent divergence of this sex chromosome system [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Pucholt P, Wright AE, Conze LL, Mank JE, Berlin S. Recent sex chromosome divergence despite ancient dioecy in the willow Salix viminalis. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:19912001. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx144&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d6089e1710">55</a>], as well as the preservative effects of haploid selection on genes expressed in plant reproductive tissues. Nevertheless, seven protein-coding genes on the corresponding Z-linked scaffolds with known products are missing from the W assembly. Using a translated BLAST search of these proteins to the corresponding Z-linked scaffolds and considering a minimum query coverage of 80%, we inferred that at least two of them (os02g0180000 on scaffold 163 and TIR on scaffold 225) have likely been pseudogenized on the W. These results suggest that gene loss can occur very quickly, even in nascent sex chromosome systems.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Candidate sex determination genes in <i>S. viminalis</i>
</h3><p>We scanned for genes unique to the <i>S. viminalis</i> W chromosome, or without preserved synteny to the Z homolog, as potential candidates for sex-determining loci in <i>S. viminalis</i>. We recovered several genes located on the W-linked scaffold 148 that could possibly be involved in this role, including WOX1, two genes in tandem of the two-component response regulator implicated in phytohormone signaling, ARR5 and ARR17, and three pre-mRNA splicing factor RNA helicases of the ESP3 gene family (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#Tab1">1</a>). We could not recover ortholog copies of ARR17 and ESP3_2 in the genome, or evidence for a pseudogene in the Z chromosome, suggesting that they could have originated through either a translocation to the W or gene loss on the Z.
</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Genes on W chromosome scaffolds 148 and 211 with non-preserved synteny relative to the homologous region on the Z chromosome. Orthologs were searched with BLASTP using an <i>e</i> value threshold of 110<sup>3</sup> and 75% minimum sequence identity</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>DEAD-box ATP-dependent RNA helicase homologs are known to regulate the formation of male gametophytes in rice [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Li X, Gao X, Wei Y, et al. Rice APOPTOSIS INHIBITOR5 coupled with two DEAD-box adenosine 5-triphosphate-dependent RNA helicases regulates tapetum degeneration. Plant Cell. 2011;23:141634. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1105/tpc.110.082636&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2280">88</a>] and have been recently identified in the candidate sex-determining region of grapevine [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Badouin H, Velt A, Gindraud F et al. The wild grape genome sequence provides insights into the transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during grape domestication. bioRxiv. 20202020.01.07.897082. doi:&#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.01.07.897082&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2283">89</a>]. WOX1 is a WUSCHEL-related homeobox protein, involved in the cytokinin regulatory pathway that coordinates stem cell proliferation with differentiation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Somssich M, Je BI, Simon R, Jackson D. CLAVATA-WUSCHEL signaling in the shoot meristem. Development. 2016;143:323848. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1242/dev.133645&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2286">90</a>]. The <i>Silene latifolia</i> homolog of WOX1, SlWUS1, is also sex-linked on the X chromosome with the homologous copy in the Y chromosome likely lost [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Kazama Y, Nishihara K, Bergero R et al. SlWUS1; an X-linked gene having no homologous Y-linked copy in Silene latifolia. G3. 2012;2:12691278. doi:&#xA;https://doi.org/10.1534/g3.112.003749&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2292">91</a>]. ARR proteins are one of the final targets of the cytokinin signaling system, which is known to play important roles in flower development and floral sex differentiation in several plant species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Wybouw B, De Rybel B. Cytokinin - a developing story. Trends Plant Sci. 2019;24:17785. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.tplants.2018.10.012&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2296">92</a>]. In particular, a C-type cytokinin response regulator that acts as a dominant suppressor of carpel development, resulting in female lethality, was specifically identified as the possible male sex-determining gene in kiwifruit [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Akagi T, Henry IM, Ohtani H, et al. A Y-encoded suppressor of feminization arose via lineage-specific duplication of a cytokinin response regulator in kiwifruit. Plant Cell. 2018;30:78095. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1105/tpc.17.00787&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2299">93</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Akagi T, Pilkington SM, Varkonyi-Gasic E, et al. Two Y-chromosome-encoded genes determine sex in kiwifruit. Nature Plants. 2019;5:8019. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41477-019-0489-6&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2302">94</a>], and a regulator of the cytokinin metabolism is also a major potential candidate for sex determination in grapevine [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Badouin H, Velt A, Gindraud F et al. The wild grape genome sequence provides insights into the transition from dioecy to hermaphroditism during grape domestication. bioRxiv. 20202020.01.07.897082. doi:&#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/2020.01.07.897082&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2305">89</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Coito JL, Ramos MJ, Cunha J, et al. VviAPRT3 and VviFSEX: two genes involved in sex specification able to distinguish different flower types in Vitis. Front Plant Sci. 2017;8:98. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2017.00098&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2308">95</a>]. Interestingly, within the Salicaceae family, sex-linked polymorphisms in the poplar genome map to a small region on chromosome 19 that includes ARR17 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Geraldes A, Hefer CA, Capron A, et al. Recent Y chromosome divergence despite ancient origin of dioecy in poplars (Populus). Mol Ecol. 2015;24:324356. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/mec.13126&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2311">43</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="McKown AD, Klpt J, Guy RD, et al. Sexual homomorphism in dioecious trees: extensive tests fail to detect sexual dimorphism in Populus. Sci Rep. 2017;7:1831. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41598-017-01893-z&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2315">96</a>]. Similarly, the sex-determining region of the purple willow, <i>S. purpurea</i>, appears to contain palindromic sequences of ARR17 that could be involved in sex determination [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Zhou R, Macaya-Sanz D, Carlson CH, et al. A willow sex chromosome reveals convergent evolution of complex palindromic repeats. Genome Biol. 2020;21:38. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/s13059-020-1952-4&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2321">97</a>].</p><p>Taken together, these observations suggest that cytokinin regulators are likely candidates for major sex-determining genes in <i>S. viminalis</i>, and it is possible that phytohormone signaling mediated by cytokinin regulators plays an important role in the sex determination cascade of this species. Additionally, it is reasonable to speculate on the evolutionary convergence in the control of genetic sex determination in plants as similar genes are likely being independently recruited in different plant families for these functions. Further functional and comparative studies will help to elucidate if these mechanisms are shared among the Salicaceae or, alternatively, evolved independently in the different groups.</p><p>It is worth noting that dioecy evolved early in the Salicaceae lineage in which <i>S. viminalis</i> is embedded and is shared by most members of the clade [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Cronk QC, Needham I, Rudall PJ. Evolution of catkins: inflorescence morphology of selected Salicaceae in an evolutionary and developmental context. Front Plant Sci. 2015;6:1030. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2015.01030&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2336">98</a>]. This means that the standard model for the evolution of sex chromosomes in plants, which assumes an immediate hermaphrodite ancestor, may not be applicable. The model posits two linked mutations encoding male and female sterility [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Charlesworth B, Charlesworth D. A model for the evolution of dioecy and gynodioecy. Am Nat. 1978;112:97597. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.2307/2460344&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2339">99</a>] as the progenitor of sex chromosomes, and this model has received some empirical support [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Harkess A, Zhou J, Xu C, et al. The asparagus genome sheds light on the origin and evolution of a young Y chromosome. Nat Commun. 2017;8:1279. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-017-01064-8&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2342">47</a>]. However, the ancient dioecy found in Salicaceae and the observation of small and heterogeneous levels of divergence in the basket willow [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Pucholt P, Wright AE, Conze LL, Mank JE, Berlin S. Recent sex chromosome divergence despite ancient dioecy in the willow Salix viminalis. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:19912001. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx144&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2345">55</a>] and poplar [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Geraldes A, Hefer CA, Capron A, et al. Recent Y chromosome divergence despite ancient origin of dioecy in poplars (Populus). Mol Ecol. 2015;24:324356. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/mec.13126&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2349">43</a>] sex chromosomes are difficult to reconcile with this two-gene model, although it is of course still possible. Indeed, recent work has pointed out alternative sex determination mechanisms in flowering plants, either determined by a single gene as in the case of <i>Diospyros</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Akagi T, Henry IM, Tao R, Comai L. A Y-chromosome-encoded small RNA acts as a sex determinant in persimmons. Science. 2014;346:64650. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1126/science.1257225&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2355">46</a>] or, as in <i>Cucumis</i> and <i>Mercurialis</i>, as a polygenic trait controlled by several genes distributed across different chromosomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Boualem A, Troadec C, Camps C, et al. A cucurbit androecy gene reveals how unisexual flowers develop and dioecy emerges. Science. 2015;350:68891. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1126/science.aac8370&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2364">100</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Cossard GG, Pannell JR. Enhanced leaky sex expression is an adaptive plastic response to pollen limitation in the dioecious plant Mercurialis annua. bioRxiv. 2019703009. doi:&#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/703009&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2368">101</a>]. The Salicaceae family with its young sex chromosomes derived from ancient dioecy therefore provides a valuable comparative system to elucidate this process.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>Here, we use multiple types of single-molecule sequencing to assemble the genome of the basket willow, <i>S. viminalis</i>, and used this to reveal the earliest stages of sex chromosome evolution. This approach allows us unprecedented power to phase our data, allowing us to resolve Z and W haplotypes at this early stage of divergence. Our results suggest that the SDR is of limited size and divergence, and we recover no evidence that recombination suppression is due to inversions in this region. Even at this early stage of divergence, we see evidence of pseudogenization and the accumulation of repetitive elements in the SDR, suggesting that these processes occur very swiftly after recombination ceases. Furthermore, we found W-linked genes involved in cytokinin regulation, suggesting that phytohormone signaling could be important in the sex determination cascade in <i>S. viminalis</i>. In total, our results shed new light on the fundamental process of sex chromosome formation.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Plant material and DNA extraction</h3><p>Fresh young leaves (approximately 200mg) were sampled from a female and a male <i>S. viminalis</i> (accession 78183 and 81084, respectively), described in [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Berlin S, Trybush SO, Fogelqvist J, et al. Genetic diversity, population structure and phenotypic variation in European Salix viminalis L. (Salicaceae). Tree Genet Genomes. 2014;10:1595610. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/s11295-014-0782-5&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2401">57</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Hallingbck HR, Fogelqvist J, Powers SJ, et al. Association mapping in Salix viminalis L. (Salicaceae) - identification of candidate genes associated with growth and phenology. Global Change Biol Bioenergy. 2016;8:67085. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/gcbb.12280&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2404">102</a>], and DNA was extracted following a CTAB protocol described in [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Pucholt P, Wright AE, Conze LL, Mank JE, Berlin S. Recent sex chromosome divergence despite ancient dioecy in the willow Salix viminalis. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:19912001. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx144&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2407">55</a>]. In brief, approximately 200mg fresh leaves were snap-frozen and pulverized. To every sample, 950l of extraction buffer (100mM Tris-HCl pH7.58, 25mM EDTA, 2M NaCl, 2% (w/v) CTAB, 2% (w/v) PVP K30, 5% (w/v) PVPP, 50g/ml RNAse) was added, and the sample was thoroughly mixed before incubation for 30min at 65C. Subsequently, 300l chloroform to isoamyl alcohol 24:1 was added, the sample mixed and centrifuged for 10min at 13,000rpm, the supernatant was transferred to a new tube, and the process was repeated. 1.5 volumes of ice-cold isopropanol were added to the supernatant followed by an incubation overnight at 20C. After centrifugation for 10min at 13,000rpm at 4C, the supernatant was removed and the pellet rinsed with chilled 100% EtOH followed by another centrifugation of 5min at 13,000rpm at 4C. The supernatant was then removed and the DNA was air-dried before it was dissolved in 100l TE buffer (10mM Tris-HCl, 1mM EDTA). DNA concentration was assessed by Qubit 3.0 Fluorometer (Thermo Fisher Scientific).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">PacBio long-read library preparation and sequencing</h3><p>A single SMRT-bell library with 20kb insert size was constructed from 10g of pure high-molecular weight DNA from one <i>S. viminalis</i> female (accession 78183) according to the manufacturers protocol (Pacific Biosciences). This library was sequenced on 48 SMRT cells using P5-C3 chemistry, and 4-h movies were captured for each SMRT cell using the PacBio RSII sequencing platform (Pacific Biosciences). Primary analysis and error correction of the raw data were done using SMRT Portal (Pacific Biosciences). After filtering, the mean read length was 8924bp (longest read was 61kbp) and a total of ~19.2Gbp of data were recovered.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">10 Genomics Chromium linked-reads library preparation and sequencing</h3><p>For both accessions (78183 and 81084), sequencing libraries were prepared from 0.75ng DNA using the Chromium TM Genome Library preparation kit according to the CG00022_Chromium Genome Reagent Kit User Guide_RevA. The library preparation was performed according to the manufacturers instructions with the exception that 0.75ng was used for library preparation instead of 1.25ng recommended by the manufacturers instructions. This was done to account for the smaller genome size of <i>S. viminalis</i> compared to the human genome for which the protocol was optimized. The libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeqX with a paired-end 150-bp read length using v2.5 sequencing chemistry (Illumina Inc.), resulting in ~58Gb of data with a mean molecule length of ~40kb.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">DNA extraction and short-read Illumina sequencing</h3><p>We generated additional Illumina sequencing data for the female accession 78183, the same accession used to assemble the reference genome. DNA was extracted from fresh leaves using the Fast DNA Kit (MP Biomedicals) according to the manufacturers instructions. Two libraries with 165 and 400bp insert size respectively were generated with the TruSeq DNA v2 kit (manual #15005180) following the manufacturers protocol and sequenced on one lane each with Illumina HiSeq2000, 100-bp paired-end read length, and v3 chemistry generating ~28Gb of bases (AdditionalFile<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S1).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Reference genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>Falcon v0.4.2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Chin CS, Peluso P, Sedlazeck FJ, et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat Methods. 2016;13:10504. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nmeth.4035&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2452">103</a>] was used to assemble the sub-reads from 48 SMRT cells. The primary contigs from this first draft assembly were then polished using Quiver from the Pacific Biosciences SMRT suite (v2.3.0) with the PacBio reads. The resulting assembly was then corrected with Pilon v 1.17 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Walker BJ, Abeel T, Shea T, et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One. 2014;9:e112963. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0112963&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2455">104</a>] using both Illumina libraries from the same individual at 80 and 53 coverage. In addition, a 10 Genomics assembly for the same female individual was also obtained using the pseudohap-style output of Supernova v2.0.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Weisenfeld NI, Kumar V, Shah P, Church DM, Jaffe DB. Direct determination of diploid genome sequences. Genome Res. 2017;27:75767. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.214874.116&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2458">105</a>]. This 10 Genomics assembly and the PacBio assembly were then merged using Quickmerge v20160905 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Chakraborty M, Baldwin-Brown JG, Long AD, Emerson JJ. Contiguous and accurate de novo assembly of metazoan genomes with modest long read coverage. Nucleic Acids Res. 2016;44:e147. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkw654&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2461">106</a>], increasing the assembly size by ~8Mb. Finally, the preads (corrected PacBio reads obtained after the first step of Falcon assembly) and the Supernova pseudohap assembly were used to scaffold the merged assembly using LINKS v1.8.4 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Warren RL, Yang C, Vandervalk BP, et al. LINKS: scalable, alignment-free scaffolding of draft genomes with long reads. GigaScience. 2015;4:35. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/s13742-015-0076-3&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2464">107</a>]. Finally, we corrected some homozygous SNPs and small insertions and deletions in the assembly using Long Ranger v2.1.2 with the 10 Genomics Chromium reads of the same female individual.</p><p>Annotation of the <i>S. viminalis</i> reference genome was performed with MAKER v3.00.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Holt C, Yandell M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics. 2011;12:491. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2105-12-491&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2473">60</a>]. The MAKER pipeline was run twice; first, based on protein and RNA sequence data only (later used to train ab initio software) and a second time combining evidence data and ab initio predictions. High-confidence protein sequences were collected from the Uniprot database [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Magrane M, UniProt C. UniProt Knowledgebase: a hub of integrated protein data. Database. 2011;2011:bar009. doi:&#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/database/bar009&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2476">108</a>], for proteins belonging to the Swissprot section that contain only manually annotated and reviewed curations (downloaded on August 2016), and two other specific protein sets from <i>Salix suchowensis</i> and <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>. Furthermore, to support gene predictions, we also used selected libraries of RNA-seq data from our previous studies collected from vegetative (leaf) and sex-specific reproductive tissue (catkin) from both female and male individuals [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Pucholt P, Wright AE, Conze LL, Mank JE, Berlin S. Recent sex chromosome divergence despite ancient dioecy in the willow Salix viminalis. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:19912001. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx144&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2486">55</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Darolti I, Wright AE, Pucholt P, Berlin S, Mank JE. Slow evolution of sex-biased genes in the reproductive tissue of the dioecious plant Salix viminalis. Mol Ecol. 2018;27:694708. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/mec.14466&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2489">61</a>]. As the basis for the construction of gene models, we combined ab initio predictions from three sources (Augustus v2.7 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Stanke M, Keller O, Gunduz I, Hayes A, Waack S, Morgenstern B. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 2006;34:W4359. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkl200&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2492">109</a>], GeneMark_ES_ET v4.3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Ter-Hovhannisyan V, Lomsadze A, Chernoff YO, Borodovsky M. Gene prediction in novel fungal genomes using an ab initio algorithm with unsupervised training. Genome Res. 2008;18:197990. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.081612.108&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2495">110</a>], and SNAP [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Korf I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics. 2004;5:59. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2105-5-59&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2498">111</a>]). GeneMark_ES_ET was self-trained with the genome sequence. To train Augustus and SNAP, we first ran the MAKER pipeline the first time to create a profile using the protein evidence along with RNA-seq data. Both Augustus and SNAP were then trained with a selected set of genes from this initial evidence-based annotation. We excluded genes with an Annotation Edit Distance (AED) score equal to 1 to avoid potentially false annotations. Functional inference for genes and transcripts was performed using the translated CDS features of each coding transcript. Protein sequences were searched with BLAST in the Uniprot/Swissprot reference dataset in order to retrieve gene names and protein functions as well as in the InterProscan v5.7-48 database to retrieve additional annotations from different sources.</p><p>We created a repeat library with an in-house pipeline using RepeatModeler v1.0.8 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 112" title="Smit AFA, Hubley R. RepeatModeler Open-1.0. 2015. &#xA;http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR112" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2504">112</a>]. Identification of repeat sequences in the genome was performed using RepeatMasker v4.0.3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 113" title="Smit AFA, Hubley R, Green P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0. 2015. &#xA;http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR113" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2507">113</a>] and RepeatRunner [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 114" title="Yandell M. Comparative genomics library - RepeatRunner. 2006. &#xA;http://www.yandell-lab.org/software/repeatrunner.html&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR114" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2510">114</a>]. tRNAs were predicted with tRNAscan v1.3.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 115" title="Lowe TM, Eddy SR. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 1997;25:95564. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/25.5.955&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR115" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2513">115</a>], and broadly conserved ncRNAs were predicted with the Infernal package [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 116" title="Nawrocki EP, Eddy SR. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics. 2013;29:29335. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btt509&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR116" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2516">116</a>] using the RNA family database Rfam v11 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 117" title="Nawrocki EP, Burge SW, Bateman A, et al. Rfam 12.0: updates to the RNA families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 2015;43:D1307. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gku1063&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR117" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2520">117</a>]. The genome assembly and annotation were converted in EMBL format using EMBLmyGFF3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 118" title="Norling M, Jareborg N, Dainat J. EMBLmyGFF3: a converter facilitating genome annotation submission to European Nucleotide Archive. BMC Research Notes. 2018;11:584. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/s13104-018-3686-x&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR118" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2523">118</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Identification of allelic scaffolds in single-molecule de novo assemblies</h3><p>The linked-reads technology of 10 Genomics uses a large number of barcoded microdroplets (GEMs) to capture long-range information over long, single-DNA molecules, enabling the assembly through repetitive regions and to resolve heterozygous haplotypes from a diploid genome. Linked reads for the female and male accessions were assembled with Supernova v2.0.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Weisenfeld NI, Kumar V, Shah P, Church DM, Jaffe DB. Direct determination of diploid genome sequences. Genome Res. 2017;27:75767. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.214874.116&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2534">105</a>]. Fully phased heterozygous haplotypes, together with non-phased sequence (nominally homozygous), were obtained using the megabubbles-style output and a minimum sequence length of 1kb. With this output style, Supernova generates an individual FASTA record for each homologous phased haplotype without mixing maternal and paternal alleles in the same sequence. Diploid assemblies were soft-masked with RepeatMasker v4.0.7 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 113" title="Smit AFA, Hubley R, Green P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0. 2015. &#xA;http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR113" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2537">113</a>] with the RMBlast v2.6.0+ search engine and using our custom <i>S. viminalis</i> repeat library generated during genome annotation.</p><p>We used sequence alignments in order to identify homologous haplotypes in our single-molecule assemblies. A repeat-masked assembly is first aligned to itself with LAST v926 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 119" title="Kiebasa SM, Wan R, Sato K, Horton P, Frith MC. Adaptive seeds tame genomic sequence comparison. Genome Res. 2011;21:48793. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.113985.110&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR119" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2546">119</a>] using the sensitive DNA seeding MAM4 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 120" title="Frith MC, No L. Improved search heuristics find 20,000 new alignments between human and mouse genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2014;42:e59. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gku104&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR120" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2549">120</a>] and masking of repeats during alignment with the -cR11 option. To avoid false matches caused by repetitive sequences and paralogous scaffolds, orthologous alignments were generated with last-split and alignments mostly comprised of masked sequence were then discarded with last-postmask. Scaffolds were considered to represent allelic variants in the assembly if the overlap exceeded 25% of sequence length after repeat masking, and with sequence identity &gt;80% to other longer scaffolds.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Anchoring scaffolds to <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>
</h3><p>Pairwise alignments between <i>P. trichocarpa</i> v10.1 (downloaded from PopGenie v3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Sjdin A, Street NR, Sandberg G, Gustafsson P, Jansson S. The Populus Genome Integrative Explorer (PopGenIE): a new resource for exploring the Populus genome. New Phytologist. 2009;182:101325. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1469-8137.2009.02807.x&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2567">62</a>]) and our <i>S. viminalis</i> assembly were generated from repeat-masked genomic sequence using LAST v926 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 119" title="Kiebasa SM, Wan R, Sato K, Horton P, Frith MC. Adaptive seeds tame genomic sequence comparison. Genome Res. 2011;21:48793. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.113985.110&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR119" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2573">119</a>]. We first prepared an index of the poplar genome using the sensitive DNA seeding MAM4 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 120" title="Frith MC, No L. Improved search heuristics find 20,000 new alignments between human and mouse genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2014;42:e59. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gku104&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR120" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2576">120</a>], using the masking repeat option -cR11 during alignment. A suitable substitution and gap frequencies matrix were then determined with last-train, using parameters --revsym --matsym --gapsym -C2. Alignments were made with lastal, using the parameters -m100 -C2 followed by last-split m1 to find 1-to-many willow-poplar orthologous matches. Finally, alignments (within scaffolds) that were composed primarily of masked sequence were ignored using last-postmask, and scaffolds with less than an overall of 10% of aligned sequence were discarded. One-to-one willow-poplar alignments were made by swapping both sequences and repeating the orthology search as above.</p><p>Neighboring alignments with &lt;10kb gap lengths were linked into a single path, and the longest tiling path was used to assign scaffolds to poplar chromosomes. Forward or reverse scaffold orientation relative to poplar chromosomes was similarly obtained requiring that the total length of one alignment direction was &gt;70% compared to the other orientation; otherwise, the original orientation was kept. If the longest tiling path for a particular scaffold did not agree with its overall alignment path on the poplar chromosome, the scaffold was marked as unlocalized.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Preprocessing of Illumina reads</h3><p>Whole-genome DNA sequencing reads were quality assessed with FastQC v0.11.5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 121" title="Andrews S. FastQC: a quality control tool for high throughput sequence data. 2016. &#xA;http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR121" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2590">121</a>] and preprocessed with BBTools v37.02 bbduk [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 122" title="Bushnell B. BBMap short read aligner, and other bioinformatic tools. 2016. &#xA;https://jgi.doe.gov/data-and-tools/bbtools/bb-tools-user-guide/&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR122" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2593">122</a>] to remove adapter sequences, to trim regions with average quality scores below Q10 from both ends of reads, and to filter out reads aligning to PhiX-174 genome (a commonly used spike-in control in Illumina sequencing runs). After filtering, read-pairs were excluded from downstream analyses if either read had an average quality score&lt;Q20 or was &lt;50 bases in length. The same criteria for quality assessment and filtering were used for RNA-seq data.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Coverage and polymorphism analysis</h3><p>Alignments to the genome assembly were performed with BWA v0.7.15-r1140 using the MEM [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 123" title="Li H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Nat Genet. 2013;1303(3997)." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR123" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2604">123</a>] algorithm and default options. General processing of SAM/BAM files was performed with SAMtools v1.6 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 124" title="Li H, Handsaker B, Wysoker A, et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics. 2009;25:20789. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btp352&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR124" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2607">124</a>], and duplicated reads were flagged with biobambam v2.0.72 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 125" title="Tischler G, Leonard S. biobambam: tools for read pair collation based algorithms on BAM files. Source Code for Biol Med. 2014;9:2078. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1751-0473-9-13&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR125" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2610">125</a>] after alignment. Per-site coverage was computed with the SAMtools depth command after filtering out reads with mapping quality Q3 that map to multiple locations, reads with secondary alignments, and duplicated reads. We then calculated the effective coverage value per scaffold and in non-overlapping windows of 10kb, as the mean per-site coverage of every site in that class. To account for the differences in the overall coverage between individuals, the coverage data were normalized for the median coverage value of each individual in the respective class.</p><p>Polymorphism analyses were conducted using the same filters as above. Read alignments were then converted to nucleotide profiles with the sam2pro program of mlRho [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 126" title="Haubold B, Pfaffelhuber P, Lynch M. mlRho - a program for estimating the population mutation and recombination rates from shotgun-sequenced diploid genomes. Molecular Ecol. 2010;19(Suppl 1):27784. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-294X.2009.04482.x&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR126" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2616">126</a>]. Only sites with a per-site coverage  5 and a SNP called for bi-allelic sites with a minor allele frequency  30% within an individual were analyzed. The average SNP density per scaffold, and window, was calculated as the number of SNPs divided by the number of sites that passed the coverage threshold of 5 for the respective class.</p><p>In order to avoid infinitely high numbers associated with log<sub>2</sub> 0 when calculating the log<sub>2</sub> difference of coverage or SNP density between females and males, we added a small number (0.1) to each value. The 95% confidence intervals for the sliding window distributions were estimated from the mean bootstrap values with resampling of 1000 random sets of 25 windows from autosomes. We excluded the entirety of chromosome 15 (the sex chromosome), including the PAR, in the bootstrapping procedure to avoid potential linkage effects resulting from the SDR.</p><p>To identify potentially W-linked scaffolds in the assembly, we proceeded as above and calculated the log<sub>2</sub> F:M coverage differences for each scaffold. All scaffolds where the normalized female coverage was &lt;10% of the normalized whole-genome coverage were excluded. This is a conservative approach because of the difficulty associated with mapping to highly repetitive potential W-linked scaffolds. These scaffolds are therefore likely to remain undetected. Scaffolds were considered W-linked if the log<sub>2</sub> F:M coverage difference was &gt;95% the genome average.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Identification of structural variations</h3><p>We used the linked-read data of 10 Genomics sequencing to search for large structural variations in the sex-determining region of the female assembly, as long-range information (average molecule length of ~40kb) can provide accurate sequencing information spanning the region around a breakpoint, even if breakpoints are in regions that are inherently difficult to assemble, for example, in areas enriched for repetitive sequence. To facilitate the interpretation of structural variants that could span adjacent scaffolds, we concatenated all scaffolds anchored to chromosome 15 into a single pseudo-chromosome, preserving scaffold ordering, separated by runs of 100 null nucleotides (N). We then used Long Ranger (10 Genomics), which employs barcode-aware read alignments for the identification of large-scale structural variations using the 10 linked-reads library of the same female used in the assembly (accession 78183). Read mapping used the full genome assembly; however, structural variants were called specifically on the pseudo-chromosome 15.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Quantification of gene expression</h3><p>Preprocessed RNA-seq reads [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Pucholt P, Wright AE, Conze LL, Mank JE, Berlin S. Recent sex chromosome divergence despite ancient dioecy in the willow Salix viminalis. Mol Biol Evol. 2017;34:19912001. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx144&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2650">55</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Darolti I, Wright AE, Pucholt P, Berlin S, Mank JE. Slow evolution of sex-biased genes in the reproductive tissue of the dioecious plant Salix viminalis. Mol Ecol. 2018;27:694708. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/mec.14466&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2653">61</a>] were filtered for rRNA using Bowtie v2.3.2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 127" title="Langmead B, Salzberg SL. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat Methods. 2012;9:3579. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nmeth.1923&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR127" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2656">127</a>], and the SILVA release 128 database of LSU and SSU NR99 rRNAs [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 128" title="Quast C, Pruesse E, Yilmaz P, et al. The SILVA ribosomal RNA gene database project: improved data processing and web-based tools. Nucleic Acids Res. 2013;41:D5906. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gks1219&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR128" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2659">128</a>]. Filtered reads were then aligned to the reference assembly using HISAT2 v2.1.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 129" title="Kim D, Langmead B, Salzberg SL. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat Methods. 2015;12:35760. &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nmeth.3317&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/10.1186/s12915-020-00808-1#ref-CR129" id="ref-link-section-d6089e2662">129</a>] with options --no-mixed --no-discordant. The resulting alignments for each library were sorted and merged by individual and by tissue (catkin and leaves) with SAMtools v1.6 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 124" title="Li H, Handsaker B, Wysoker A, et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools.
